src="/w/load.php?debug=false&amp;lang=en&amp;modules=html5shiv&amp;only=scripts&amp;skin=vector&amp;sync=1"></script><![endif]-->\n</head>\n<body class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject page-Money_laundering rootpage-Money_laundering skin-vector action-view">\t\t<div id="mw-page-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Money laundering</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Dirty money" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Dirty_Money_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Dirty Money (disambiguation)">Dirty Money (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p><p><b>Money laundering</b> is the act of concealing the transformation of profits from illegal activities and <a href="/wiki/Corruption" title="Corruption">corruption</a> into ostensibly "legitimate" assets.<sup id="cite_ref-Duhaimes_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duhaimes-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The dilemma of illicit activities is accounting for the origin of the proceeds of such activities without raising the suspicion of law enforcement agencies. Accordingly, considerable time and effort is put into devising strategies which enable the safe use of those proceeds without raising unwanted suspicion. Implementing such strategies is generally called money laundering. After money has been suitably laundered or "cleaned", it can be used in the mainstream economy for accumulation of wealth, such as acquisitions of properties, or otherwise spent. Law enforcement agencies of many jurisdictions have set up sophisticated systems in an effort to detect suspicious transactions or activities, and many have set up international cooperative arrangements to assist each other in these endeavors.\nIn a number of legal and regulatory systems, the term "money laundering" has become <a href="/wiki/Conflation" title="Conflation">conflated</a> with other forms of <a href="/wiki/Financial_crime" title="Financial crime">financial</a> and <a href="/wiki/White-collar_crime" title="White-collar crime">business crime</a>, and is sometimes used more generally to include misuse of the financial system (involving things such as securities, <a href="/wiki/Digital_currency" title="Digital currency">digital currencies</a>, credit cards, and traditional currency), including <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_financing" title="Terrorism financing">terrorism financing</a> and evasion of <a href="/wiki/International_sanctions" title="International sanctions">international sanctions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Most anti-money laundering laws openly conflate money laundering (which is concerned with <i>source</i> of funds) with terrorism financing (which is concerned with <i>destination</i> of funds) when regulating the financial system.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some countries treat obfuscation of sources of money as also constituting money laundering, whether it is  intentional or by merely using financial systems or services that do not identify or track sources or destinations. Other countries define money laundering in such a way as to include money from activity that <i>would have been</i> a crime in that country, even if the activity was legal where the actual conduct occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" <span class="toctext">Digital money and Money laundering</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Laws against money laundering were created to use against organized crime during the period of <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">Prohibition in the United States</a> during the 1930s. <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">Organized crime</a> received a major boost from Prohibition and a large source of new funds that were obtained from illegal sales of alcohol. The successful prosecution of <a href="/wiki/Al_Capone" title="Al Capone">Al Capone</a> on <a href="/wiki/Tax_evasion" title="Tax evasion">tax evasion</a> brought in a new emphasis by the state and law enforcement agencies to track and confiscate money, but existing laws against tax evasion could not be used once gangsters started paying their taxes.\n</p><p>In the 1980s, the <a href="/wiki/War_on_drugs" title="War on drugs">war on drugs</a> led governments again to turn to money-laundering rules in an attempt to seize proceeds of <a href="/wiki/Drug-related_crime" title="Drug-related crime">drug crimes</a> in order to catch the organizers and individuals running drug empires. It also had the benefit from a law enforcement point of view of turning rules of evidence upside down. Law enforcers normally have to prove an individual is guilty to get a conviction. But with money laundering laws, money can be confiscated and it is up to the individual to prove that the source of funds is legitimate if they want the funds back. This makes it much easier for law enforcement agencies and provides for much lower <a href="/wiki/Burden_of_proof_(law)" title="Burden of proof (law)">burdens of proof</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2001, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Patriot_Act" title="Patriot Act">Patriot Act</a> in the U.S. and similar legislation worldwide, led to a new emphasis on money laundering laws to combat <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_financing" title="Terrorism financing">terrorism financing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Seven_(G7)" class="mw-redirect" title="Group of Seven (G7)">Group of Seven (G7)</a> nations used the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force_on_Money_Laundering" title="Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering">Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering</a> to put pressure on governments around the world to increase surveillance and monitoring of financial transactions and share this information between countries. Starting in 2002, governments around the world upgraded money laundering laws and surveillance and monitoring systems of financial transactions. Anti money laundering regulations have become a much larger burden for <a href="/wiki/Financial_institution" title="Financial institution">financial institutions</a> and enforcement has stepped up significantly. During 2011\xe2\x80\x932015 a number of major banks faced ever-increasing fines for breaches of money laundering regulations. This included <a href="/wiki/HSBC" title="HSBC">HSBC</a>, which was fined $1.9 billion in December 2012, and <a href="/wiki/BNP_Paribas" title="BNP Paribas">BNP Paribas</a>, which was fined $8.9 billion in July 2014 by the U.S. government.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_bnp_admits_guilt_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_bnp_admits_guilt-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Many countries introduced or strengthened border controls on the amount of cash that can be carried and introduced central transaction reporting systems where all financial institutions have to report all financial transactions electronically. For example, in 2006, Australia set up the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Transaction_Reports_and_Analysis_Centre" title="Australian Transaction Reports and Analysis Centre">AUSTRAC</a> system and required the reporting of all financial transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Money-laundering.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Money-laundering.svg/220px-Money-laundering.svg.png" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Money-laundering.svg/330px-Money-laundering.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Money-laundering.svg/440px-Money-laundering.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="443" data-file-height="410" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Money-laundering.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Placing "dirty" money in a service company, where it is layered with legitimate income and then integrated into the flow of money, is a common form of money laundering.</div></div></div><p> The conversion or transfer of property, the concealment or disguising of the nature of the proceeds, the acquisition, possession or use of property, knowing that these are derived from criminal activity and participate or assist the movement of funds to make the proceeds appear legitimate is money laundering.\n</p><p>Money obtained from certain crimes, such as <a href="/wiki/Extortion" title="Extortion">extortion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_trafficking" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug trafficking">drug trafficking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Illegal_gambling" class="mw-redirect" title="Illegal gambling">illegal gambling</a> is "dirty" and needs to be "cleaned" to appear to have been derived from legal activities, so that banks and other financial institutions will deal with it without suspicion. Money can be laundered by many methods which vary in complexity and sophistication.\n</p><p>Money laundering involves three steps: The first involves introducing cash into the financial system by some means ("placement"); the second involves carrying out complex financial transactions to camouflage the illegal source of the cash ("layering"); and finally, acquiring wealth generated from the transactions of the illicit funds ("integration"). Some of these steps may be omitted, depending upon the circumstances. For example, non-cash proceeds that are already in the financial system would not need to be placed.<sup id="cite_ref-chasing_dirty_money_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chasing_dirty_money-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Treasury Department">United States Treasury Department</a>:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Money laundering is the process of making illegally-gained proceeds (i.e., "dirty money") appear legal (i.e., "clean"). Typically, it involves three steps: placement, layering, and integration. First, the illegitimate funds are furtively introduced into the legitimate financial system. Then, the money is moved around to create confusion, sometimes by wiring or transferring through numerous accounts. Finally, it is integrated into the financial system through additional transactions until the "dirty money" appears "clean".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Money laundering can take several forms, although most methods can be categorized into one of a few types. These include "bank methods, smurfing [also known as structuring], currency exchanges, and double-invoicing".<sup id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Structuring" title="Structuring">Structuring</a>: Often known as <i><a href="/wiki/Smurfing_(financial_crime)" class="mw-redirect" title="Smurfing (financial crime)">smurfing</a></i>, this is a method of placement whereby cash is broken into smaller deposits of money, used to defeat suspicion of money laundering and to avoid anti-money laundering reporting requirements. A sub-component of this is to use smaller amounts of cash to purchase bearer instruments, such as money orders, and then ultimately deposit those, again in small amounts.<sup id="cite_ref-NDIC_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDIC-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Bulk cash smuggling: This involves physically smuggling cash to another jurisdiction and depositing it in a financial institution, such as an <a href="/wiki/Offshore_bank" title="Offshore bank">offshore bank</a>, with greater <a href="/wiki/Bank_secrecy" title="Bank secrecy">bank secrecy</a> or less rigorous money laundering enforcement.<sup id="cite_ref-National_Money_Laundering_Threat_Assessment_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Money_Laundering_Threat_Assessment-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Cash-intensive businesses: In this method, a business typically expected to receive a large proportion of its revenue as cash uses its accounts to deposit criminally derived cash. Such enterprises often operate openly and in doing so generate cash revenue from incidental legitimate business in addition to the illicit cash \xe2\x80\x93 in such cases the business will usually claim all cash received as legitimate earnings. Service businesses are best suited to this method, as such enterprises have little or no  <a href="/wiki/Variable_cost" title="Variable cost">variable costs</a> and/or a large ratio between revenue and variable costs, which makes it difficult to detect discrepancies between revenues and costs. Examples are parking structures,  <a href="/wiki/Strip_club" title="Strip club">strip clubs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanning_salon" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanning salon">tanning salons</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Car_wash" title="Car wash">car washes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amusement_arcade" title="Amusement arcade">arcades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bar" title="Bar">bars</a>, restaurants, and casinos.</li>\n<li>Trade-based laundering: This involves under- or over-valuing <a href="/wiki/Invoice" title="Invoice">invoices</a> to disguise the movement of money.<sup id="cite_ref-Achilles_Heel_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Achilles_Heel-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> For example, the art market has been accused of being an ideal vehicle for money laundering due to several unique aspects of art such as the subjective value of artworks as well as the secrecy of auction houses about the identity of the buyer and seller.<sup id="cite_ref-Has_the_Art_Market_Become_an_Unwitting_Partner_in_Crime?_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Has_the_Art_Market_Become_an_Unwitting_Partner_in_Crime?-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Shell companies and trusts: Trusts and shell companies disguise the true owners of money. Trusts and corporate vehicles, depending on the jurisdiction, need not disclose their true owner.  Sometimes referred to by the slang term <i>rathole</i>, though that term usually refers to a person acting as the fictitious owner rather than the business entity.<sup id="cite_ref-FATF_threat_assessment_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FATF_threat_assessment-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-private-eye.co.uk_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-private-eye.co.uk-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Round-tripping_(finance)" title="Round-tripping (finance)">Round-tripping</a>: Here, money is deposited in a <a href="/wiki/Controlled_foreign_corporation" title="Controlled foreign corporation">controlled foreign corporation</a> offshore, preferably in a <a href="/wiki/Tax_haven" title="Tax haven">tax haven</a> where minimal records are kept, and then shipped back as a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment" title="Foreign direct investment">foreign direct investment</a>, exempt from taxation. A variant on this is to transfer money to a law firm or similar organization as funds on account of fees, then to cancel the retainer and, when the money is remitted, represent the sums received from the lawyers as a legacy under a will or proceeds of litigation.<sup id="cite_ref-private-eye.co.uk_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-private-eye.co.uk-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Bank capture: In this case, money launderers or criminals buy a controlling interest in a bank, preferably in a jurisdiction with weak money laundering controls, and then move money through the bank without scrutiny.</li>\n<li>Casinos: In this method, an individual walks into a casino and buys chips with illicit cash. The individual will then play for a relatively short time. When the person cashes in the chips, they will expect to take payment in a check, or at least get a receipt so they can claim the proceeds as gambling winnings.<sup id="cite_ref-National_Money_Laundering_Threat_Assessment_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Money_Laundering_Threat_Assessment-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Other gambling: Money is spent on gambling, preferably on high odds games. One way to minimize risk with this method is to bet on every possible outcome of some event that has many possible outcomes, so no outcome(s) have short odds, and the bettor will lose only the <a href="/wiki/Vigorish" title="Vigorish">vigorish</a> and will have one or more winning bets that can be shown as the source of money. The losing bets will remain hidden.</li>\n<li>Real estate: Someone purchases real estate with illegal proceeds and then sells the property.<sup id="cite_ref-private-eye.co.uk_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-private-eye.co.uk-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> To outsiders, the proceeds from the sale look like legitimate income. Alternatively, the price of the property is manipulated: the seller agrees to a contract that underrepresents the value of the property, and receives criminal proceeds to make up the difference.<sup id="cite_ref-FATF_threat_assessment_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FATF_threat_assessment-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Black salaries: A company may have unregistered employees without written contracts and pay them cash salaries. Dirty money might be used to pay them.<sup id="cite_ref-Underground_Economy_Issues._Ontario_Construction_Secretariat_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Underground_Economy_Issues._Ontario_Construction_Secretariat-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tax_amnesty" title="Tax amnesty">Tax amnesties</a>: For example, those that legalize unreported assets and cash in tax havens.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Life insurance business: Assignment of policies to unidentified third parties and for which no plausible reasons can be ascertained.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Money_Laundering_by_Region">Money Laundering by Region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Money Laundering by Region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In Latin America, money laundering is mainly linked to drug trafficking activities and to having connections with criminal activity, such as crimes that have to do with arms trafficking, human trafficking, extortion, blackmail, smuggling, and acts of corruption of people linked to governments, such as bribery, which are more common in Latin American countries. There is a relationship between corruption and money laundering in developing countries. The economic power of Latin America increases rapidly and without support, these fortunes being of illicit origin have the appearance of legally acquired profits. With regard to money laundering, the ultimate goal of the process is to integrate illicit capital into the general economy and transform it into licit goods and services. \n</p><p>The money laundering practice uses various channels to legalize everything achieved through illegal practices. As such, it has different techniques depending on the country where this illegal operation is going to be carried out:\n</p>\n<ul><li>In Colombia, the laundering of billions of dollars, which come from drug trafficking, is carried out through imports of contraband from the parallel exchange market.</li>\n<li>In Central America in countries such as Guatemala and Honduras, money laundering continues to increase in the absence of adequate legislation and regulations in these countries. Money laundering activities in Costa Rica have experienced substantial growth, especially using large-scale currency smuggling and investments of drug cartels in real estate, within the tourism sector. Furthermore, the Colon Free Zone, in Panama, continues to be the area of operations for money laundering where cash is exchanged for products of different nature that are then put up for sale at prices below those of production for a return fast of the capital.</li>\n<li>In Mexico, the preferred techniques continue to be the smuggling of currency abroad, in addition to electronic transfers, bank drafts with Mexican banks and operations in the parallel exchange market.</li>\n<li>Money Laundering in the Caribbean countries continues to be a serious problem that seems to be very dangerous. Specifically, in Antigua, the Dominican Republic, Jamaica, Saint Vincent and the Grenadines. Citizens of the Dominican Republic who have been involved in money laundering in the United States, use companies that are dedicated to transferring funds sent to the Dominican Republic in amounts of less than $ 10,000 under the use of false names. Moreover, in Jamaica, multi-million dollar asset laundering cases were discovered through telephone betting operations abroad. Thousands of suspicious transactions have been detected in French overseas territories. Free trade zones such as Aruba, meanwhile, remain the preferred areas for money laundering. The offshore banking centers, the secret bank accounts and the tourist complexes are the channels through which the launderers whiten the proceeds of the illicit money.</li></ul>\n<p>Casinos continue to attract organizations that deal with money laundering. Aruba and the Netherlands Antilles, the Cayman Islands, Colombia, Mexico, Panama and Venezuela are considered high priority countries in the region, due to the strategies used by the washers.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact_in_the_region">Economic impact in the region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Economic impact in the region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The practice of money laundering, among other economic and financial crimes have been better in infiltrating into the economic and political structures of most developing countries therefore resulting to political instability and economic digression.\n</p><p>Money laundering is still a great concern for the financial services industry.  About 50% of the money laundering incidents in Latin America were reported by organizations in the financial sector. According to PwC\xe2\x80\x99s 2014 global economic crime survey in Latin America only 2.8% of respondents in Latin America claimed suffering Anti-Trust/Competition Law incidents, compared to 5.2% of respondents globally.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>It has been shown that money laundering has an impact on the financial behavior and macroeconomic performance of the industrialized countries. In these countries the macroeconomic consequences of money laundering are transmitted through several channels. Thus, money laundering complicates the formulation of economic policies. It is assumed that the proceeds of criminal activities are laundered by means of the notes and coins in circulation of the monetary substitutes.\n</p><p>The laundering causes disproportionate changes in the relative prices of assets which implies that resources are allocated inefficiently; and, therefore may have negative implications for economic growth, apparently money laundering is associated with a lower economic growth.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Office_of_National_Drug_Control_Policy" title="Office of National Drug Control Policy">Office of National Drug Control Policy</a> of the United States estimates that only in that country, sales of narcotic drugs represent about 57,000 millions of dollars annually and most of these transactions are made in cash<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jurisprudence">Jurisprudence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Jurisprudence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Money laundering has been increasing. A key factor behind the growing money laundering is ineffective enforcement of money laundering laws locally. Perhaps because of the lack of importance that has been given to the subject, since the 21st century started, there was not jurisprudence regarding the laundering of money or assets, or the conversion or transfer of goods. Which is even worse, the laws of the Latin American countries have really not dealt with their study in a profound way, as it is an issue that concerns the whole world and is the subject of seminars, conferences and academic analysis in different regions of the planet. Now a new figure that is being called the Economic Criminal Law is being implemented, which should be implemented in modern societies, which has been inflicted enormous damage to the point of affecting the general economy of the states. Even though, developing countries have responded and continue to respond, through legislative measures, to the problem of money laundering, at national level, however, money launderers, have taken advantage of the lax regulatory environment, vulnerable financial systems along with the continued  civil and political unrest of most the developing countries.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnitude">Magnitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Magnitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Many regulatory and governmental authorities issue estimates each year for the amount of money laundered, either worldwide or within their national economy. In 1996, a spokesperson for the IMF estimated that 2\xe2\x80\x935% of the worldwide global economy involved laundered money.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force_on_Money_Laundering" title="Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering">Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering</a> (FATF), an intergovernmental body set up to combat money laundering, stated, "Overall, it is absolutely impossible to produce a reliable estimate of the amount of money laundered and therefore the FATF does not publish any figures in this regard."<sup id="cite_ref-FATF_FAQ_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FATF_FAQ-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Academic commentators have likewise been unable to estimate the volume of money with any degree of assurance.<sup id="cite_ref-chasing_dirty_money_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chasing_dirty_money-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Various estimates of the scale of global money laundering are sometimes repeated often enough to make some people regard them as factual\xe2\x80\x94but no researcher has overcome the inherent difficulty of measuring an actively concealed practice.\n</p><p>Regardless of the difficulty in measurement, the amount of money laundered each year is in the <a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billions</a> of US dollars and poses a significant policy concern for governments.<sup id="cite_ref-chasing_dirty_money_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chasing_dirty_money-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As a result, governments and international bodies have undertaken efforts to deter, prevent, and apprehend money launderers. Financial institutions have likewise undertaken efforts to prevent and detect transactions involving dirty money, both as a result of government requirements and to avoid the reputational risk involved. Issues relating to money laundering have existed as long as there have been <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">large scale criminal enterprises</a>. Modern anti-money laundering laws have developed along with the modern <a href="/wiki/War_on_Drugs" class="mw-redirect" title="War on Drugs">War on Drugs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In more recent times anti-money laundering legislation is seen as adjunct to the financial crime of <a href="/wiki/Terrorist_financing" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrorist financing">terrorist financing</a> in that both crimes usually involve the transmission of funds through the financial system (although money laundering relates to where the money has come <i>from</i>, and terrorist financing relating to where the money is going <i>to</i>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronic_money">Electronic money</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Electronic money">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In theory, <a href="/wiki/Electronic_money" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic money">electronic money</a> should provide as easy a method of transferring value without revealing identity as untracked banknotes, especially wire transfers involving anonymity-protecting numbered bank accounts.  In practice, however, the record-keeping capabilities of Internet service providers and other network resource maintainers tend to frustrate that intention.  While some <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency" title="Cryptocurrency">cryptocurrencies</a> under recent development have aimed to provide for more possibilities of transaction anonymity for various reasons, the degree to which they succeed\xe2\x80\x94and, in consequence, the degree to which they offer benefits for money laundering efforts\xe2\x80\x94is controversial. Solutions such as <a href="/wiki/Zcash" title="Zcash">ZCash</a> and Monero are examples of cryptocurrencies that provide unlinkable anonymity via proofs and/or obfuscation of information (<a href="/wiki/Ring_signature" title="Ring signature">ring signatures</a>). Such currencies could find use in online illicit services.\n</p><p>In 2013, Jean-Loup Richet, a research fellow at <a href="/wiki/ESSEC" class="mw-redirect" title="ESSEC">ESSEC</a> ISIS, surveyed new techniques that cybercriminals were using in a report written for the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_on_Drugs_and_Crime" title="United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime">United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean-Loup_Richet_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean-Loup_Richet-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> A common approach was to use a <a href="/wiki/Digital_currency_exchanger" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital currency exchanger">digital currency exchanger</a> service which converted dollars into a digital currency called <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Reserve" title="Liberty Reserve">Liberty Reserve</a>, and could be sent and received anonymously. The receiver could convert the Liberty Reserve currency back into cash for a small fee. In May 2013, the US authorities shut down Liberty Reserve charging its founder and various others with money laundering.<sup id="cite_ref-Wired_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another increasingly common way of laundering money is to use online gaming. In a growing number of online games, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Second_Life" title="Second Life">Second Life</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/World_of_Warcraft" title="World of Warcraft">World of Warcraft</a>,</i> it is possible to <a href="/wiki/Gold_farming" title="Gold farming">convert money into virtual goods, services, or virtual cash</a> that can later be converted back into money.<sup id="cite_ref-Wired.uk_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wired.uk-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reverse_money_laundering">Reverse money laundering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reverse money laundering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Reverse money laundering is a process that disguises a legitimate source of funds that are to be used for illegal purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-REVERSE_LAUNDERING_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-REVERSE_LAUNDERING-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> It is usually perpetrated for the purpose of financing terrorism<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> but can be also used by criminal organizations that have invested in legal businesses and would like to withdraw legitimate funds from official circulation. Unaccounted cash received via disguising financial transactions is not included in official financial reporting and could be used to evade taxes, hand in bribes and pay "under-the-table" salaries.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> For example, in an affidavit filed on 24 March 2014 in United States District Court, Northern California, San Francisco Division, FBI special agent Emmanuel V. Pascau alleged that several people associated with the Chee Kung Tong organization, and California State Senator <a href="/wiki/Leland_Yee" title="Leland Yee">Leland Yee</a>, engaged in reverse money laundering activities.\n</p><p>The problem of such fraudulent encashment practices  (<i>obnalichka</i> in Russian) has become acute in Russia and other countries of the former Soviet Union. The Eurasian Group on Combating Money Laundering and Financing of Terrorism (EAG) reported that the Russian Federation, Ukraine, Turkey, Serbia, Kyrgyzstan, Uzbekistan, Armenia and Kazakhstan have encountered a substantial shrinkage of tax base and shifting money supply balance in favor of cash. These processes have complicated planning and management of the economy and contributed to the growth of the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Shadow economy">shadow economy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Combating"><span id="Anti-money_laundering"></span> Combating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Combating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Anti-money laundering (AML) is a term mainly used in the financial and legal industries to describe the legal controls that require <a href="/wiki/Financial_institution" title="Financial institution">financial institutions</a> and other regulated entities to prevent, detect, and report money laundering activities. Anti-money laundering guidelines came into prominence globally as a result of the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial Action Task Force">Financial Action Task Force</a> (FATF) and the promulgation of an international framework of anti-money laundering standards.<sup id="cite_ref-about_the_FATF_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about_the_FATF-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> These standards began to have more relevance in 2000 and 2001, after FATF began a process to publicly identify countries that were deficient in their anti-money laundering laws and international cooperation, a process colloquially known as "<a href="/wiki/Name_and_shame" title="Name and shame">name and shame</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FATF_NCCT_initiative_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FATF_NCCT_initiative-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An effective AML program requires a jurisdiction to criminalise money laundering, giving the relevant regulators and police the powers and tools to investigate; be able to share information with other countries as appropriate; and require financial institutions to identify their customers, establish risk-based controls, keep records, and report suspicious activities.<sup id="cite_ref-Money_Laundering_FAQ_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Money_Laundering_FAQ-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Strict background checks are necessary to combat as many money launderers escape by investing through complex ownership and company structures. Banks can do that but a proper surveillance is required but on the Government side to reduce this. <sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminalization">Criminalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Criminalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small><i>(May 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The elements of the crime of money laundering are set forth in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_Against_Illicit_Traffic_in_Narcotic_Drugs_and_Psychotropic_Substances" title="United Nations Convention Against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances">United Nations Convention Against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances</a> and <a href="/wiki/Convention_against_Transnational_Organized_Crime" class="mw-redirect" title="Convention against Transnational Organized Crime">Convention against Transnational Organized Crime</a>. It is defined as knowingly engaging in a financial transaction with the proceeds of a crime for the purpose of concealing or disguising the illicit origin of the property from governments.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_role_of_financial_institutions">The role of financial institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The role of financial institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While banks operating in the same country generally have to follow the same anti-money laundering laws and regulations, financial institutions all structure their anti-money laundering efforts slightly differently.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Today, most financial institutions globally, and many non-financial institutions, are required to identify and <a href="/wiki/Suspicious_activity_report" title="Suspicious activity report">report transactions of a suspicious nature</a> to the financial intelligence unit in the respective country. For example, a bank must verify a customer\'s identity and, if necessary, monitor transactions for suspicious activity. This is often termed as "<a href="/wiki/Know_your_customer" title="Know your customer">know your customer</a>". This means knowing the identity of the customer and understanding the kinds of transactions in which the customer is likely to engage. By knowing one\'s customers, financial institutions can often identify unusual or suspicious behaviour, termed anomalies, which may be an indication of money laundering.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bank employees, such as tellers and customer account representatives, are trained in anti-money laundering and are instructed to report activities that they deem suspicious. Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Anti-money_laundering_software" title="Anti-money laundering software">anti-money laundering software</a> filters customer data, classifies it according to level of suspicion, and inspects it for anomalies. Such anomalies include any sudden and substantial increase in funds, a large withdrawal, or moving money to a bank secrecy jurisdiction. Smaller transactions that meet certain criteria may also be flagged as suspicious. For example, structuring can lead to flagged transactions. The software also flags names on government "blacklists" and transactions that involve countries hostile to the host nation. Once the software has mined data and flagged suspect transactions, it alerts bank management, who must then determine whether to file a report with the government.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Value_of_enforcement_costs_and_associated_privacy_concerns">Value of enforcement costs and associated privacy concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Value of enforcement costs and associated privacy concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The financial services industry has become more vocal about the rising costs of anti-money laundering regulation and the limited benefits that they claim it brings.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> One commentator wrote that "[w]ithout facts, [anti-money laundering] legislation has been driven on rhetoric, driving by ill-guided activism responding to the need to be "seen to be doing something" rather than by an objective understanding of its effects on predicate crime. The social panic approach is justified by the language used\xe2\x80\x94we talk of the battle against terrorism or the war on drugs".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> magazine has become increasingly vocal in its criticism of such regulation, particularly with reference to countering terrorist financing, referring to it as a "costly failure", although it concedes that other efforts (like reducing identity and credit card fraud) may still be effective at combating money laundering.<sup id="cite_ref-economist_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is no precise measurement of the costs of regulation balanced against the harms associated with money laundering,<sup id="cite_ref-Levi_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levi-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and given the evaluation problems involved in assessing such an issue, it is unlikely that the effectiveness of terror finance and money laundering laws could be determined with any degree of accuracy.<sup id="cite_ref-Levi_terrorism_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levi_terrorism-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i> estimated the annual costs of anti-money laundering efforts in Europe and North America at US$5 billion in 2003, an increase from US$700 million in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Government-linked economists have noted the significant negative effects of money laundering on economic development, including undermining domestic capital formation, depressing growth, and diverting capital away from development.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Because of the intrinsic uncertainties of the amount of money laundered, changes in the amount of money laundered, and the cost of anti-money laundering systems, it is almost impossible to tell which anti-money laundering systems work and which are more or less cost effective.\n</p><p>Besides economic costs to implement anti-money-laundering laws, improper attention to <a href="/wiki/Information_privacy" title="Information privacy">data protection</a> practices may entail disproportionate costs to individual privacy rights. In June 2011, the data-protection advisory committee to the European Union issued a report on data protection issues related to the prevention of money laundering and terrorist financing, which identified numerous transgressions against the established legal framework on privacy and data protection.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The report made recommendations on how to address money laundering and terrorist financing in ways that safeguard personal privacy rights and data protection laws.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, groups such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_Liberties_Union" title="American Civil Liberties Union">American Civil Liberties Union</a> have expressed concern that money laundering rules require banks to report on their own customers, essentially conscripting private businesses "into agents of the surveillance state".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many countries are obligated by various international instruments and standards, such as the 1988 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_Against_Illicit_Traffic_in_Narcotic_Drugs_and_Psychotropic_Substances" title="United Nations Convention Against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances">United Nations Convention Against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances</a>, the 2000 <a href="/wiki/Convention_against_Transnational_Organized_Crime" class="mw-redirect" title="Convention against Transnational Organized Crime">Convention against Transnational Organized Crime</a>, the 2003 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_against_Corruption" title="United Nations Convention against Corruption">United Nations Convention against Corruption</a>, and the recommendations of the 1989 <a href="/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force_on_Money_Laundering" title="Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering">Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering</a> (FATF) to enact and enforce money laundering laws in an effort to stop narcotics trafficking, international organised crime, and corruption. Mexico, which has faced a significant increase in violent crime, established anti-money laundering controls in 2013 to curb the underlying crime issue.<sup id="cite_ref-Mallen_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mallen-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_organizations">Global organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Global organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Formed in 1989 by the <a href="/wiki/G7" class="mw-redirect" title="G7">G7</a> countries, the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force_on_Money_Laundering" title="Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering">Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering</a> (FATF) is an intergovernmental body whose purpose is to develop and promote an international response to combat money laundering. The FATF Secretariat is housed at the headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> in Paris. In October 2001, FATF expanded its mission to include combating the financing of terrorism. FATF is a policy-making body that brings together legal, financial, and law enforcement experts to achieve national legislation and regulatory AML and CFT reforms. As of  2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> its membership consists of 36 countries and territories and two regional organizations.  FATF works in collaboration with a number of international bodies and organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> These entities have observer status with FATF, which does not entitle them to vote, but permits them full participation in plenary sessions and working groups.<sup id="cite_ref-FATF_member_FAQ_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FATF_member_FAQ-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>FATF has developed 40 recommendations on money laundering and 9 special recommendations regarding terrorist financing. FATF assesses each member country against these recommendations in published reports. Countries seen as not being sufficiently compliant with such recommendations are subjected to financial sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>FATF\'s three primary functions with regard to money laundering are:\n</p>\n<ol><li>Monitoring members\xe2\x80\x99 progress in implementing anti-money laundering measures,</li>\n<li>Reviewing and reporting on laundering trends, techniques, and countermeasures, and</li>\n<li>Promoting the adoption and implementation of FATF anti-money laundering standards globally.</li></ol>\n<p>The FATF currently comprises 34 member jurisdictions and 2 regional organisations, representing most major financial centres in all parts of the globe.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_on_Drugs_and_Crime" title="United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime">United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime</a> maintains the <i>International Money Laundering Information Network</i>, a website that provides information and software for anti-money laundering data collection and analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> has a website that provides policy advice and best practices to governments and the private sector on anti-money laundering issues.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The Basel AML Index is an independent annual ranking that assesses the risk of money laundering and terrorist financing around the world. <sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-money-laundering_measures_by_region">Anti-money-laundering measures by region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Anti-money-laundering measures by region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Many jurisdictions adopt a list of specific predicate crimes for money laundering prosecutions, while others criminalize the proceeds of any serious crimes.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Afghanistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(November 2011)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The Financial Transactions and Reports Analysis Center of Afghanistan (FinTRACA) was established as a Financial Intelligence Unit (FIU) under the Anti Money Laundering and Proceeds of Crime Law passed by decree late in 2004. The main purpose of this law is to protect the integrity of the Afghan financial system and to gain compliance with international treaties and conventions. The Financial Intelligence Unit is a semi-independent body that is administratively housed within the Central Bank of Afghanistan (Da Afghanistan Bank). The main objective of FinTRACA is to deny the use of the Afghan financial system to those who obtained funds as the result of illegal activity, and to those who would use it to support terrorist activities.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To meet its objectives, the FinTRACA collects and analyzes information from a variety of sources. These sources include entities with legal obligations to submit reports to the FinTRACA when a suspicious activity is detected, as well as reports of cash transactions above a threshold amount specified by regulation. Also, FinTRACA has access to all related Afghan government information and databases. When the analysis of this information supports the supposition of illegal use of the financial system, the FinTRACA works closely with law enforcement to investigate and prosecute the illegal activity. FinTRACA also cooperates internationally in support of its own analyses and investigations and to support the analyses and investigations of foreign counterparts, to the extent allowed by law. Other functions include training of those entities with legal obligations to report information, development of laws and regulations to support national-level AML objectives, and international and regional cooperation in the development of AML typologies and countermeasures.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Australia has adopted a number of strategies to combat money laundering, which mirror those of a majority of western countries. The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Transaction_Reports_and_Analysis_Centre" title="Australian Transaction Reports and Analysis Centre">Australian Transaction Reports and Analysis Centre</a> (<a href="/wiki/AUSTRAC" class="mw-redirect" title="AUSTRAC">AUSTRAC</a>) is Australia\'s financial intelligence unit to combat money laundering and terrorism financing, which requires financial institutions and other \'cash dealers\' in Australia to report to it suspicious cash or other transactions and other specific information. The <a href="/wiki/Attorney-General%27s_Department_(Australia)" title="Attorney-General&#39;s Department (Australia)">Attorney-General\'s Department</a> maintains a list of <a href="/wiki/Outlawed_terror_organisations_in_Australia" title="Outlawed terror organisations in Australia">outlawed terror organisations</a>. It is an offense to materially support or be supported by such organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> It is an offence to open a bank account in Australia in a false name,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and rigorous procedures must be followed when new bank accounts are opened. \n</p><p>The <i>Anti-Money Laundering and Counter-Terrorism Financing Act 2006</i> (Cth) (AML/CTF Act) is the principal legislative instrument, although there are also offence provisions contained in Division 400 of the <i>Criminal Code Act 1995</i> (Cth). Upon its introduction, it was intended that the AML/CTF Act would be further amended by a second tranche of reforms extending to designated non-financial businesses and professions (DNFBPs) including, inter alia, lawyers, accountants, jewellers and real estate agents; however, those further reforms have yet to be progressed.\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proceeds_of_Crime_Act_1987&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Proceeds of Crime Act 1987 (page does not exist)">Proceeds of Crime Act 1987</a></i> (Cth) imposes criminal penalties on a person who engages in money laundering, and allows for confiscation of property. The principal objects of the Act are set out in s.3(1):\n</p>\n<ul><li>to deprive persons of the proceeds of, and benefits derived from the commission of offences,</li>\n<li>to provide for the forfeiture of property used in or in connection with the commission of such offences, and</li>\n<li>to enable <a href="/wiki/Australian_Securities_and_Investments_Commission" title="Australian Securities and Investments Commission">law enforcement authorities</a> to effectively trace such proceeds, benefits and property.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bangladesh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first anti-money laundering legislation in Bangladesh was the <i>Money Laundering Prevention Act, 2002</i>. It was replaced by the <i>Money Laundering Prevention Ordinance 2008</i>. Subsequently, the ordinance was repealed by the <i>Money Laundering Prevention Act, 2009</i>. In 2012, government again replace it with the <i>Money Laundering Prevention Act, 2012</i><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In terms of section 2, "Money Laundering means \xe2\x80\x93 (i)  knowingly moving, converting, or transferring proceeds of crime or property involved in an offence for the following purposes:- (1) concealing or disguising the illicit nature, source,  location, ownership or control of the proceeds of crime; or (2) assisting any person involved in the commission of the predicate offence to evade the legal consequences of such offence;  (ii)   smuggling money or property earned through legal or illegal means to a foreign country; (iii)  knowingly transferring or remitting the proceeds of crime to a foreign country or remitting or bringing them into Bangladesh from a foreign country with the intention of hiding or disguising its illegal source; or (iv) concluding or attempting to conclude financial transactions in such a manner so as to reporting requirement under this Act may be avoided;(v) converting or moving or transferring property with the intention to instigate or assist for committing a predicate offence; (vi) acquiring, possessing or using any property, knowing that such property is the proceeds of a predicate offence; (vii) performing such activities so as to the illegal source of the proceeds of crime may be concealed or disguised; (viii) participating in, associating with, conspiring, attempting, abetting, instigate or counsel to commit any offences mentioned above.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To prevent these Illegal uses of money, the Bangladesh government has introduced the Money Laundering Prevention Act. The Act was last amended in the year 2009 and all the financial institutes are following this act. Till today there are 26 circulars issued by Bangladesh Bank under this act. To prevent money laundering, a banker must do the following:\n</p>\n<ul><li>While opening a new account, the account opening form should be duly filled up by all the information of the customer.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Know_your_customer" title="Know your customer">KYC</a> must be properly filled.</li>\n<li>The Transaction Profile (TP) is mandatory for a client to understand his/her transactions. If needed, the TP must be updated at the client\'s consent.</li>\n<li>All other necessary papers should be properly collected along with the National ID card.</li>\n<li>If any suspicious transaction is noticed, the Branch Anti Money Laundering Compliance Officer (BAMLCO) must be notified and accordingly the Suspicious Transaction Report (STR) must be filled out.</li>\n<li>The cash department should be aware of the transactions. It must be noted if suddenly a big amount of money is deposited in any account. Proper documents are required if any client does this type of transaction.</li>\n<li>Structuring, over/ under invoicing is another way to do money laundering. The foreign exchange department should look into this matter cautiously.</li>\n<li>If any account has a transaction over 1 million taka in a single day, it must be reported in a cash transaction report (CTR).</li>\n<li>All bank officials must go through all the 26 circulars and use them.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1991, the Proceeds of Crime (Money Laundering) Act was brought into force in Canada to give legal effect to the former <a href="/wiki/FATF" class="mw-redirect" title="FATF">FATF</a> Forty Recommendations by establishing record keeping and client identification requirements in the financial sector to facilitate the investigation and prosecution of money laundering offences under the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Code_(Canada)" title="Criminal Code (Canada)">Criminal Code</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Controlled_Drugs_and_Substances_Act" title="Controlled Drugs and Substances Act">Controlled Drugs and Substances Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AML_Legislation_in_Canada_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AML_Legislation_in_Canada-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2000, the Proceeds of Crime (Money Laundering) Act was amended to expand the scope of its application and to establish a financial intelligence unit with national control over money laundering, namely <a href="/wiki/FINTRAC" class="mw-redirect" title="FINTRAC">FINTRAC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AML_Legislation_in_Canada_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AML_Legislation_in_Canada-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2001, the scope of the Proceeds of Crime (Money Laundering) Act was again expanded by amendments enacted under the Anti-Terrorism Act with the objective of deterring terrorist activity by cutting off sources and channels of funding used by terrorists in response to <a href="/wiki/9/11" class="mw-redirect" title="9/11">9/11</a>.  The Proceeds of Crime (Money Laundering) Act was renamed the Proceeds of Crime (Money Laundering) and Terrorist Financing Act.<sup id="cite_ref-AML_Legislation_in_Canada_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AML_Legislation_in_Canada-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2006, the Proceeds of Crime (Money Laundering) and Terrorist Financing Act was further amended, in part, in response to pressure from the FATF for Canada to tighten its money laundering and financing of terrorism legislation.  The amendments expanded the client identification, record-keeping and reporting requirements for certain organizations and included new obligations to report attempted suspicious transactions and outgoing and incoming international electronic fund transfers, undertake risk assessments and implement written compliance procedures in respect of those risks.<sup id="cite_ref-AML_Legislation_in_Canada_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AML_Legislation_in_Canada-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The amendments also enabled greater money laundering and terrorist financing intelligence-sharing among enforcement agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-AML_Legislation_in_Canada_63-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AML_Legislation_in_Canada-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Canada, casinos, money service businesses, notaries, accountants, banks, securities brokers, life insurance agencies, real estate salespeople and dealers in precious metals and stones are subject to the reporting and record keeping obligations under the Proceeds of Crime (Money Laundering) and Terrorist Financing Act.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The fourth and latest iteration of the EU\xe2\x80\x99s anti-money laundering directive (AMLD IV) was published on 5 June 2015, after clearing its last legislative stop at the European Parliament. The new directive brings the EU\xe2\x80\x99s anti-money laundering laws more in line with the US\xe2\x80\x99s, which is welcome news for financial institutions that are operating in both jurisdictions.<sup id="cite_ref-pwc.com_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pwc.com-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://complyadvantage.com/blog/5mld-fifth-anti-money-laundering-directive/">The Fifth Money Laundering Directive (5MLD)</a> will come into force on the 10th January 2020. It will address a number of weaknesses in the European Union\xe2\x80\x99s AML/CFT regime that have come to light since the enactment of the Fourth Money Laundering Directive AMLD IV).\n</p><p>Lack of harmonization in AML requirements between the US and EU has complicated the compliance efforts of global institutions that are looking to standardize the Know Your Customer (KYC) component of their AML programs across key jurisdictions. AMLD IV promises to better align the AML regimes by adopting a more risk-based approach compared to its predecessor, AMLD III.<sup id="cite_ref-pwc.com_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pwc.com-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Certain components of the directive, however, go beyond current requirements in both the EU and US, imposing new implementation challenges on banks. For instance, more public officials are brought within the scope of the directive, and EU member states are required to establish new registries of \xe2\x80\x9cbeneficial owners\xe2\x80\x9d (i.e., those who ultimately own or control each company) which will impact banks. AMLD IV became effective 25 June 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prevention_of_Money_Laundering_Act,_2002" title="Prevention of Money Laundering Act, 2002">Prevention of Money Laundering Act, 2002</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_Directorate" title="Enforcement Directorate">Enforcement Directorate</a></div>\n<p>In 2002, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_India" title="Parliament of India">Parliament of India</a> passed an <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">act</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Prevention_of_Money_Laundering_Act,_2002" title="Prevention of Money Laundering Act, 2002">Prevention of Money Laundering Act, 2002</a>. The main objectives of this act are to prevent money-laundering as well as to provide for confiscation of property either derived from or involved in, money-laundering.<sup id="cite_ref-Prevention_of_Money_Laundering_Act,_2002_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prevention_of_Money_Laundering_Act,_2002-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Section 12 (1) describes the obligations that banks, other financial institutions, and intermediaries have to \n</p>\n<dl><dd>(a) Maintain records that detail the nature and value of transactions, whether such transactions comprise a single transaction or a series of connected transactions, and where these transactions take place within a month.</dd>\n<dd>(b) Furnish information on transactions referred to in clause (a) to the Director within the time prescribed, including records of the identity of all its clients.</dd></dl>\n<p>Section 12 (2) prescribes that the records referred to in sub-section (1) as mentioned above, must be maintained for ten years after the transactions finished. It is handled by the Indian Income Tax Department.\n</p><p>The provisions of the Act are frequently reviewed and various amendments have been passed from time to time.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most money laundering activities in India are through political parties, corporate companies and the shares market. These are investigated by the <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_Directorate" title="Enforcement Directorate">Enforcement Directorate</a> and Indian Income Tax Department.<sup id="cite_ref-ED_-_functions_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ED_-_functions-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">Government of India</a>, out of the total tax arrears of <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space: nowrap">\xe2\x82\xb9</span>2,480 billion</span> (US$35&#160;billion) about <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space: nowrap">\xe2\x82\xb9</span>1,300 billion</span> (US$18&#160;billion) pertain to money laundering and securities scam cases.<sup id="cite_ref-tax_recovery_-_india_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tax_recovery_-_india-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bank accountants must record all transactions over Rs. 1&#160;million and maintain such records for 10 years. Banks must also make cash transaction reports (CTRs) and suspicious transaction reports over Rs. 1&#160;million within 7 days of initial suspicion. They must submit their reports to the Enforcement Directorate and Income Tax Department.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>\xe2\x80\x99s legal framework for combating money laundering is contained in a patchwork of legal instruments,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> the main elements of which are:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The Corruption, Drug Trafficking and Other Serious Crimes (Confiscation of Benefits) Act (CDSA).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> This statute criminalises money laundering and imposes the requirement for persons to file suspicious transaction reports (STRs) and make a disclosure whenever physical currency or goods exceeding S$20,000 are carried into or out of Singapore.</li>\n<li>The Mutual Assistance in Criminal Matters Act (MACMA).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> This statute sets out the framework for mutual legal assistance in criminal matters.</li>\n<li>Legal instruments issued by regulatory agencies (such as the <a href="/wiki/Monetary_Authority_of_Singapore" title="Monetary Authority of Singapore">Monetary Authority of Singapore</a> (MAS),<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> in relation to financial institutions (FIs)) imposing requirements to conduct customer due diligence (CDD).</li></ul>\n<p>The term \xe2\x80\x98money laundering\xe2\x80\x99 is not used as such within the CDSA. Part VI of the CDSA criminalises the laundering of proceeds generated by criminal conduct and drug tracking via the following offences:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The assistance of another person in retaining, controlling or using the benefits of <a href="/wiki/Drug_dealing" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug dealing">drug dealing</a> or criminal conduct under an arrangement (whether by concealment, removal from jurisdiction, transfer to nominees or otherwise) [section 43(1)/44(1)].</li>\n<li>The concealment, conversion, transfer or removal from the jurisdiction, or the acquisition, possession or use of benefits of drug dealing or criminal conduct [section 46(1)/47(1)].</li>\n<li>The concealment, conversion, transfer or removal from the jurisdiction of another person\xe2\x80\x99s benefits of drug dealing or criminal conduct [section 46(2)/47(2)].</li>\n<li>The acquirement, possession or use of another person\xe2\x80\x99s benefits of drug dealing or criminal conduct [section 46(3)/47(3)].</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-Money_Laundering_Office_(Thailand)" title="Anti-Money Laundering Office (Thailand)">Anti-Money Laundering Office
