(Thailand)</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Money laundering and terrorist funding legislation in the UK is governed by four Acts of primary legislation:-\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Terrorism_Act_2000" title="Terrorism Act 2000">Terrorism Act 2000</a><sup id="cite_ref-TERRA1_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TERRA1-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anti-terrorism,_Crime_and_Security_Act_2001" title="Anti-terrorism, Crime and Security Act 2001">Anti-terrorism, Crime and Security Act 2001</a><sup id="cite_ref-ATCSA1_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATCSA1-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Proceeds_of_Crime_Act_2002" title="Proceeds of Crime Act 2002">Proceeds of Crime Act 2002</a><sup id="cite_ref-POCA1_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POCA1-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Serious_Organised_Crime_and_Police_Act_2005" title="Serious Organised Crime and Police Act 2005">Serious Organised Crime and Police Act 2005</a><sup id="cite_ref-SOCPA1_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOCPA1-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_Laundering_Regulations_2007&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Money Laundering Regulations 2007 (page does not exist)">Money Laundering Regulations 2007</a></li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/attachment_data/file/599835/Money_Laundering_Regulations_2017_-_FINAL_CONSULTATION_DRAFT_FINAL.pdf">Money Laundering Regulation, Terrorist Financing and Transfer of Funds (Information on the Payer) Regulations 2017</a></li>\n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sanctions_and_Anti-Money_Laundering_Act_2018&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sanctions and Anti-Money Laundering Act 2018 (page does not exist)">Sanctions and Anti-Money Laundering Act 2018</a><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Money Laundering Regulations are designed to protect the UK financial system, as well as preventing and detecting crime. If a business is covered by these regulations then controls are put in place to prevent it being used for money laundering.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Proceeds_of_Crime_Act_2002" title="Proceeds of Crime Act 2002">Proceeds of Crime Act 2002</a> contains the primary UK anti-money laundering legislation,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> including provisions requiring businesses within the "regulated sector" (banking, investment, money transmission, certain professions, etc.) to report to the authorities suspicions of money laundering by customers or others.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Money laundering is broadly defined in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In effect any handling or involvement with any proceeds of any crime (or monies or assets representing the proceeds of crime) can be a money laundering offence. An offender\'s possession of the proceeds of his own crime falls within the UK definition of money laundering.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The definition also covers activities within the traditional definition of money laundering, as a process that conceals or disguises the proceeds of crime to make them appear legitimate.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike certain other jurisdictions (notably the US and much of Europe), UK money laundering offences are not limited to the proceeds of serious crimes, nor are there any monetary limits. Financial transactions need no money laundering design or purpose for UK laws to consider them a money laundering offence. A money laundering offence under UK legislation need not even involve money, since the money laundering legislation covers assets of any description. In consequence, any person who commits an acquisitive crime (i.e., one that produces some benefit in the form of money or an asset of any description) in the UK inevitably also commits a money laundering offence under UK legislation.\n</p><p>This applies also to a person who, by criminal conduct, evades a liability (such as a taxation liability)\xe2\x80\x94which lawyers call "obtaining a pecuniary advantage"\xe2\x80\x94as he is deemed thereby to obtain a sum of money equal in value to the liability evaded.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The principal money laundering offences carry a maximum penalty of 14 years\' imprisonment.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Secondary regulation is provided by the Money Laundering Regulations 2003,<sup id="cite_ref-MLR1_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLR1-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> which was replaced by the Money Laundering Regulations 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-MLR2_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLR2-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> They are directly based on the EU directives 91/308/EEC, 2001/97/EC and 2005/60/EC.\n</p><p>One consequence of the Act is that solicitors, accountants, tax advisers, and insolvency practitioners who suspect (as a consequence of information received in the course of their work) that their clients (or others) have engaged in tax evasion or other criminal conduct that produced a benefit, now must report their suspicions to the authorities (since these entail suspicions of money laundering). In most circumstances it would be an offence, "tipping-off", for the reporter to inform the subject of his report that a report has been made.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> These provisions do not however require disclosure to the authorities of information received by certain professionals in privileged circumstances or where the information is subject to <a href="/wiki/Legal_professional_privilege" title="Legal professional privilege">legal professional privilege</a>. Others that are subject to these regulations include financial institutions, credit institutions, estate agents (which includes chartered surveyors), trust and company service providers, high value dealers (who accept cash equivalent to \xe2\x82\xac15,000 or more for goods sold), and casinos.\n</p><p>Professional guidance (which is submitted to and approved by the UK Treasury) is provided by industry groups including the Joint Money Laundering Steering Group,<sup id="cite_ref-JMLSG1_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JMLSG1-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> the Law Society.<sup id="cite_ref-LS1_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LS1-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and the Consultative Committee of Accountancy Bodies (CCAB).\nHowever, there is no obligation on banking institutions to routinely report monetary deposits or transfers above a specified value. Instead reports must be made of all suspicious deposits or transfers, irrespective of their value.\n</p><p>The reporting obligations include reporting suspicious gains from conduct in other countries that would be criminal if it took place in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Exceptions were later added for certain activities legal where they took place, such as <a href="/wiki/Bullfighting" title="Bullfighting">bullfighting</a> in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>More than 200,000 reports of suspected money laundering are submitted annually to authorities in the UK (there were 240,582 reports in the year ended 30 September 2010. This was an increase from the 228,834 reports submitted in the previous year).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Most of these reports are submitted by banks and similar financial institutions (there were 186,897 reports from the banking sector in the year ended 30 September 2010).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although 5,108 different organisations submitted <a href="/wiki/Suspicious_activity_report_(banking)" class="mw-redirect" title="Suspicious activity report (banking)">suspicious activity reports</a> to the authorities in the year ended 30 September 2010, just four organisations submitted approximately half of all reports, and the top 20 reporting organisations accounted for three-quarters of all reports.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The offence of failing to report a suspicion of money laundering by another person carries a maximum penalty of 5 years\' imprisonment.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On May 1, 2018, the UK House of Commons, without opposition,<sup id="cite_ref-aolmay1_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aolmay1-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> passed the Sanctions and Anti-Money Laundering Bill, which will set out the UK government\xe2\x80\x99s intended approach to exceptions and licenses when the nation becomes responsible for implementing its own sanctions and will also require notorious overseas British territory tax havens such as the Cayman Islands and the British Virgin Islands to establish public registers of the beneficial ownership of firms in their jurisdictions by the end of 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aolmay1_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aolmay1-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>  The legislation was passed by the House of Lords on May 21 and received Royal Asset on May 23.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>  However, the Act\'s public register provision is facing legal challenges from local governments in the Cayman Islands and British Virgin Islands, who argue that it violates their Constitutional sovereignty.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bureaux_de_change"><i>Bureaux de change</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Bureaux de change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>All UK <i><a href="/wiki/Bureaux_de_change" class="mw-redirect" title="Bureaux de change">Bureaux de change</a></i> are registered with <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Revenue_and_Customs" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Revenue and Customs">Her Majesty\'s Revenue and Customs</a>, which issues a trading licence for each location. Bureaux de change and <a href="/wiki/Money_transmitter" title="Money transmitter">money transmitters</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Western_Union" title="Western Union">Western Union</a> outlets, in the UK fall within the "regulated sector" and are required to comply with the Money Laundering Regulations 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-MLR2_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLR2-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Checks can be carried out by HMRC on all <a href="/wiki/Money_Service_Business" class="mw-redirect" title="Money Service Business">Money Service Businesses</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In South Africa, the Financial Intelligence Centre Act (2001) and subsequent amendments have added responsibilities to the FSB to combat money laundering.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The approach in the United States to stopping money laundering is usually broken into two areas: preventive (regulatory) measures and criminal measures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Preventive">Preventive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Preventive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In an attempt to prevent dirty money from entering the U.S. financial system in the first place, the United States Congress passed a series of laws, starting in 1970, collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Bank_Secrecy_Act" title="Bank Secrecy Act">Bank Secrecy Act</a> (BSA). These laws, contained in sections 5311 through 5332 of Title 31 of the United States Code, require <a href="/wiki/Financial_institution" title="Financial institution">financial institutions</a>, which under the current definition include a broad array of entities, including banks, credit card companies, life insurers, <a href="/wiki/Money_service_businesses" class="mw-redirect" title="Money service businesses">money service businesses</a> and broker-dealers in securities, to report certain transactions to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">United States Department of the Treasury</a>. Cash transactions in excess of a certain amount must be reported on a <a href="/wiki/Currency_transaction_report" title="Currency transaction report">currency transaction report</a> (CTR), identifying the individual making the transaction as well as the source of the cash. The law originally required all transactions of <a href="/wiki/USD" class="mw-redirect" title="USD">US$</a>5,000 or more to be reported, but due to excessively high levels of reporting the threshold was raised to US$10,000.  The U.S. is one of the few countries in the world to require reporting of all cash transactions over a certain limit, although certain businesses can be exempt from the requirement.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, financial institutions must report transaction on a <a href="/wiki/Suspicious_Activity_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="Suspicious Activity Report">Suspicious Activity Report</a> (SAR) that they deem "suspicious", defined as a knowing or suspecting that the funds come from illegal activity or disguise funds from illegal activity, that it is structured to evade BSA requirements or appears to serve no known business or apparent lawful purpose; or that the institution is being used to facilitate criminal activity. Attempts by customers to circumvent the BSA, generally by structuring cash deposits to amounts lower than US$10,000 by breaking them up and depositing them on different days or at different locations also violates the law.<sup id="cite_ref-fincen_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fincen-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The financial database created by these reports is administered by the U.S.\'s Financial Intelligence Unit (FIU), called the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crimes_Enforcement_Network" title="Financial Crimes Enforcement Network">Financial Crimes Enforcement Network</a> (FinCEN), located in Vienna, Virginia. The reports are made available to U.S. criminal investigators, as well as other FIU\'s around the globe, and FinCEN conducts computer assisted analyses of these reports to determine trends and refer investigations.<sup id="cite_ref-FinCEN_Mission_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinCEN_Mission-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The BSA requires financial institutions to engage in customer due diligence, or KYC, which is sometimes known in the parlance as <a href="/wiki/Know_your_customer" title="Know your customer">know your customer</a>. This includes obtaining satisfactory identification to give assurance that the account is in the customer\'s true name, and having an understanding of the expected nature and source of the money that flows through the customer\'s accounts. Other classes of customers, such as those with private banking accounts and those of foreign government officials, are subjected to enhanced due diligence because the law deems that those types of accounts are a higher risk for money laundering. All accounts are subject to ongoing monitoring, in which internal bank software scrutinizes transactions and flags for manual inspection those that fall outside certain parameters. If a manual inspection reveals that the transaction is suspicious, the institution should file a <a href="/wiki/Suspicious_Activity_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="Suspicious Activity Report">Suspicious Activity Report</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-roth_monograph_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roth_monograph-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The regulators of the industries involved are responsible to ensure that the financial institutions comply with the BSA. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Comptroller_of_the_Currency" title="Office of the Comptroller of the Currency">Office of the Comptroller of the Currency</a> regularly inspect banks, and may impose civil fines or refer matters for criminal prosecution for non-compliance. A number of banks have been fined and prosecuted for failure to comply with the BSA. Most famously, <a href="/wiki/Riggs_Bank" title="Riggs Bank">Riggs Bank</a>, in Washington D.C., was prosecuted and functionally driven out of business as a result of its failure to apply proper money laundering controls, particularly as it related to foreign political figures.<sup id="cite_ref-Joseph_USAB_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joseph_USAB-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the BSA, the U.S. imposes controls on the movement of currency across its borders, requiring individuals to report the transportation of cash in excess of US$10,000 on a form called Report of International Transportation of Currency or Monetary Instruments (known as a CMIR).<sup id="cite_ref-CMIR_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMIR-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Likewise, businesses, such as automobile dealerships, that receive cash in excess of US$10,000 must file a Form 8300 with the Internal Revenue Service, identifying the source of the cash.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 1 September 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crimes_Enforcement_Network" title="Financial Crimes Enforcement Network">Financial Crimes Enforcement Network</a> issued an advisory on "<a href="/wiki/Informal_value_transfer_system" title="Informal value transfer system">informal value transfer systems</a>" referencing <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Banki" title="United States v. Banki">United States v. Banki</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the United States, there are perceived consequences of anti-money laundering (AML) regulations. These <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequences" title="Unintended consequences">unintended consequences</a><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> include FinCEN\'s publishing of a list of "risky businesses," which many believe unfairly targeted money service businesses. The publishing of this list and the subsequent fall-out, banks indiscriminately <a href="/w/index.php?title=De-risking&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="De-risking (page does not exist)">de-risking</a> MSBs, is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Operation_Choke_Point" title="Operation Choke Point">Operation Choke Point</a>. The Financial Crimes Enforcement Network issued a Geographic Targeting Order to combat against illegal money laundering in the United States. This means that title insurance companies in the U.S. are required to identify the natural persons behind companies that pay all cash in residential real estate purchases over a particular amount in certain U.S. cities.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_sanctions">Criminal sanctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Criminal sanctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Money laundering has been criminalized in the United States since the <a href="/wiki/Money_Laundering_Control_Act" title="Money Laundering Control Act">Money Laundering Control Act</a> of 1986. The law, contained at section 1956 of Title 18 of the United States Code, prohibits individuals from engaging in a financial transaction with proceeds that were generated from certain specific crimes, known as "specified unlawful activities" (SUAs). The law requires that an individual specifically intend in making the transaction to conceal the source, ownership or control of the funds. There is no minimum threshold of money, and no requirement that the transaction succeeded in actually disguising the money. A "financial transaction" has been broadly defined, and need not involve a financial institution, or even a business. Merely passing money from one person to another, with the intent to disguise the source, ownership, location or control of the money, has been deemed a financial transaction under the law. The possession of money without either a financial transaction or an intent to conceal is not a crime in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Cassella_USAB_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cassella_USAB-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Besides money laundering, the law contained in section 1957 of Title 18 of the United States Code, prohibits spending more than US$10,000 derived from an SUA, regardless of whether the individual wishes to disguise it. It carries a lesser penalty than money laundering, and unlike the money laundering statute, requires that the money pass through a financial institution.<sup id="cite_ref-Cassella_USAB_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cassella_USAB-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the records compiled by the United States Sentencing Commission, in 2009, the United States Department of Justice typically convicted a little over 81,000 people; of this, approximately 800 are convicted of money laundering as the primary or most serious charge.<sup id="cite_ref-USSC_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USSC-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Anti-Drug_Abuse_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Drug Abuse Act">Anti-Drug Abuse Act</a> of 1988 expanded the definition of financial institution to include businesses such as car dealers and real estate closing personnel and required them to file reports on large currency transaction. It required verification of identity of those who purchase monetary instruments over $3,000. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Annunzio-Wylie_Anti-Money_Laundering_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Annunzio-Wylie Anti-Money Laundering Act (page does not exist)">Annunzio-Wylie Anti-Money Laundering Act</a> of 1992 strengthened sanctions for BSA violations, required so called "Suspicious Activity Reports" and eliminated previously used "Criminal Referral Forms", required verification and recordkeeping for wire transfers and established the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bank_Secrecy_Act_Advisory_Group&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bank Secrecy Act Advisory Group (page does not exist)">Bank Secrecy Act Advisory Group</a> (BSAAG). The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_Laundering_Suppression_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Money Laundering Suppression Act (page does not exist)">Money Laundering Suppression Act</a> from 1994 required banking agencies to review and enhance training, develop anti-money laundering examination procedures, review and enhance procedures for referring cases to law enforcement agencies, streamlined the <a href="/wiki/Currency_transaction_report" title="Currency transaction report">Currency transaction report</a> exemption process, required each <a href="/wiki/Money_services_business" title="Money services business">Money services business</a> (MSB) to be registered by an owner or controlling person, required every MSB to maintain a list of businesses authorized to act as agents in connection with the financial services offered by the MSB, made operating an unregistered MSB a federal crime, and recommended that states adopt uniform laws applicable to MSBs. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_Laundering_and_Financial_Crimes_Strategy_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Money Laundering and Financial Crimes Strategy Act (page does not exist)">Money Laundering and Financial Crimes Strategy Act</a> of 1998 required banking agencies to develop anti-money laundering training for examiners, required the Department of the Treasury and other agencies to develop a "National Money Laundering Strategy", created the "High Intensity Money Laundering and Related Financial Crime Area" (HIFCA) Task Forces to concentrate law enforcement efforts at the federal, state and local levels in zones where money laundering is prevalent. HIFCA zones may be defined geographically or can be created to address money laundering in an industry sector, a financial institution, or group of financial institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-hicfa_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hicfa-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Intelligence_Reform_%26_Terrorism_Prevention_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Intelligence Reform &amp; Terrorism Prevention Act (page does not exist)">Intelligence Reform &amp; Terrorism Prevention Act</a> of 2004 amended the Bank Secrecy Act to require the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> to prescribe regulations requiring certain financial institutions to report cross-border electronic transmittals of funds, if the Secretary determines that reporting is "reasonably necessary" in "anti-money laundering /combatting financing of terrorists (Anti-Money Laundering/Combating the Financing of Terrorism AML/CFT).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_cases">Notable cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Notable cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf/page1-220px-1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf/page1-330px-1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf/page1-440px-1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1275" data-file-height="1650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1998_Senate_Investigation_Contribution_Money_Laundering.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1998 investigation, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a>, Contribution Laundering/Third-Party Transfers. Includes investigation of <a href="/wiki/Gandhi_Memorial_International_Foundation" title="Gandhi Memorial International Foundation">Gandhi Memorial International Foundation</a>.</div></div></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charter_House_Bank&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Charter House Bank (page does not exist)">Charter House Bank</a>: Charter House Bank in Kenya was placed under statutory management in 2006 by the Central Bank of Kenya after it was discovered the bank was being used for money laundering activities by multiple accounts containing missing customer information. More than $1.5 billion had been laundered before the scam was uncovered.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Credit_and_Commerce_International" title="Bank of Credit and Commerce International">Bank of Credit and Commerce International</a>: Unknown amount, estimated in billions, of criminal proceeds, including drug trafficking money, laundered during the mid-1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-BCCI_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BCCI-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/BTA_Bank" title="BTA Bank">BTA Bank</a>: $6 billion of bank funds embezzled or fraudulently loaned to shell companies and offshore holdings by the banks former chairman and CEO <a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_Ablyazov" title="Mukhtar Ablyazov">Mukhtar Ablyazov</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EWHC2788_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWHC2788-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bank_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank of New York">Bank of New York</a>: US$7&#160;<a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> of Russian <a href="/wiki/Capital_flight" title="Capital flight">capital flight</a> laundered through accounts controlled by bank executives, late 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos" title="Ferdinand Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a>: Unknown amount, estimated at US$10&#160;billion of government assets laundered through banks and financial institutions in the United States, Liechtenstein, Austria, Panama, Netherlands Antilles, Cayman Islands, Vanuatu, Hong Kong, Singapore, Monaco, the Bahamas, the Vatican and Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-bernstein-realestate_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bernstein-realestate-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/HSBC" title="HSBC">HSBC</a>, in December 2012, paid a record $1.9 Billion fines for money-laundering hundreds of millions of dollars for drug traffickers, terrorists and sanctioned governments such as Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The money-laundering occurred throughout the 2000s.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberty_Reserve" title="Liberty Reserve">Liberty Reserve</a>, in May 2013, was seized by United States federal authorities for laundering $6 billion.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Works_of_Religion" title="Institute for the Works of Religion">Institute for the Works of Religion</a>: Italian authorities investigated suspected money laundering transactions amounting to US$218&#160;million made by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Works_of_Religion" title="Institute for the Works of Religion">IOR</a> to several Italian banks.<sup id="cite_ref-times-vatican_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times-vatican-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Nauru: US$70&#160;billion of Russian capital flight laundered through unregulated Nauru offshore shell banks, late 1990s<sup id="cite_ref-NY_times_shack_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY_times_shack-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sani_Abacha" title="Sani Abacha">Sani Abacha</a>: US$2\xe2\x80\x935&#160;billion of government assets laundered through banks in the UK, Luxembourg, Jersey (Channel Islands), and Switzerland, by the president of Nigeria.<sup id="cite_ref-asset_recovery_abacha_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asset_recovery_abacha-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chartered" title="Standard Chartered">Standard Chartered</a>: paid $330 million in fines for money-laundering hundreds of billions of dollars for Iran. The money-laundering took place in the 2000s and occurred for "nearly a decade to hide 60,000 transactions worth $250 billion".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Standard_Bank" title="Standard Bank">Standard Bank</a>: Standard Bank South Africa London Branch \xe2\x80\x93 The Financial Conduct Authority (FCA) has fined Standard Bank PLC (Standard Bank) \xc2\xa37,640,400 for failings relating to its anti-money laundering (AML) policies and procedures over corporate and private bank customers connected to <a href="/wiki/Politically_exposed_person" title="Politically exposed person">politically exposed persons</a> (PEPs).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/BNP_Paribas" title="BNP Paribas">BNP Paribas</a>, in June 2014,  pleaded guilty to falsifying <a href="/wiki/Business_records" class="mw-redirect" title="Business records">business records</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_(civil)" title="Conspiracy (civil)">conspiracy</a>, having violated <a href="/wiki/U.S._sanctions" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. sanctions">U.S. sanctions</a> against Cuba, Iran, and Sudan. It agreed to pay an $8.9 billion fine, the largest ever for violating U.S. sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_bnp_admits_guilt_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_bnp_admits_guilt-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/BSI_Ltd" title="BSI Ltd">BSI Bank</a>, in May 2017,  was shut down by the <a href="/wiki/Monetary_Authority_of_Singapore" title="Monetary Authority of Singapore">Monetary Authority of Singapore</a> for serious breaches of anti-money laundering requirements, poor management oversight of the bank\'s operations, and gross misconduct of some of the bank\'s staff.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Narcos" title="Narcos">Jose Franklin Jurado-Rodriguez</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a> and <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> Graduate School of Arts and Sciences Economics Department alumnus, was convicted in Luxembourg in June 1990 "in what was one of the largest drug money laundering cases ever brought in Europe"<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and the US in 1996 of money laundering for the <a href="/wiki/Cali_Cartel" title="Cali Cartel">Cali Cartel</a> kingpin <a href="/wiki/Jose_Santacruz_Londono" class="mw-redirect" title="Jose Santacruz Londono">Jose Santacruz Londono</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Jurado-Rodriguez specialized in "<a href="/wiki/Smurfing_(financial_crime)" class="mw-redirect" title="Smurfing (financial crime)">smurfing</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_money_and_Money_laundering">Digital money and Money laundering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Digital money and Money laundering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>To prevent the usage of decentralized digital money such as <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin" title="Bitcoin">Bitcoin</a> for the profit of crime and corruption, Australia is planning to strengthen the nation\'s anti-money laundering laws.  <sup id="cite_ref-ABC_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\nKnowing the characteristics of Bitcoin,<sup id="cite_ref-Bitcoin_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bitcoin-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> it is completely deterministic, protocol based and cannot be censored, may circumvent national laws using services like <a href="/wiki/Tor_(anonymity_network)" title="Tor (anonymity network)">Tor</a> to obfuscate transaction origins, and relies completely off of cryptography, not a central entity running under a <a href="/wiki/Know_your_customer" title="Know your customer">KYC</a> framework. There are several cases of criminals have cashed out a significant amount of Bitcoin after ransomware attacks, drug dealings, cyber fraud and gunrunning. <sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>   <sup id="cite_ref-express.co.uk_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-express.co.uk-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Other damages such as <a href="/wiki/The_DAO_(organization)" title="The DAO (organization)">The DAO</a> being drained of <a href="/wiki/Ethereum" title="Ethereum">Ether</a> cannot be classified as money laundering under any legal definition, as decentralized virtual environments are legally stateless and cannot be intervened with by a governing body. Such an incident has been debated as to the clear definition of money laundering in a stateless environment, leading to <a href="/wiki/Ethereum_Classic" title="Ethereum Classic">Ethereum Classic</a> to form.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bank_Secrecy_Act" title="Bank Secrecy Act">Bank Secrecy Act</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Confiscation" title="Confiscation">Confiscation</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Currency_transaction_report" title="Currency transaction report">Currency transaction report</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Customer_Identification_Program" title="Customer Identification Program">Customer Identification Program</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">Embezzlement</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force_on_Money_Laundering" title="Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering">Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Financial_Crimes_Enforcement_Network" title="Financial Crimes Enforcement Network">Financial Crimes Enforcement Network</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Global_RADAR" title="Global RADAR">Global RADAR</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gold_laundering" title="Gold laundering">Gold laundering</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hawala" title="Hawala">Hawala</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Money_trail" class="mw-redirect" title="Money trail">Money trail</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Michael_O%27Keefe_(Louisiana_politician)" title="Michael O&#39;Keefe (Louisiana politician)">Michael H. O\'Keefe</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Office_of_Foreign_Assets_Control" title="Office of Foreign Assets Control">Office of Foreign Assets Control</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Offshore_banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Offshore banking">Offshore banking</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">Organized crime</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Penny_stock_scam" class="mw-redirect" title="Penny stock scam">Penny stock scam</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Politically_exposed_person" title="Politically exposed person">Politically exposed person</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Round-tripping_(finance)" title="Round-tripping (finance)">Round-tripping (finance)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Shell_(corporation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shell (corporation)">Shell (corporation)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Terrorist_financing" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrorist financing">Terrorist financing</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/USA_PATRIOT_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="USA PATRIOT Act">USA PATRIOT Act</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/White-collar_crime" title="White-collar crime">White-collar crime</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/World_Bank_residual_model" title="World Bank residual model">World Bank residual model</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Money_laundering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em;
